<p><i><b>2001: A Space Odyssey</b></i> is a 1968 <a href="/wiki/Epic_film" title="Epic film">epic</a> <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_film" title="Science fiction film">science-fiction film</a> produced and directed by <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kubrick" title="Stanley Kubrick">Stanley Kubrick</a>. The screenplay, written by Kubrick and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>, was partially inspired by Clarke's short story "<a href="/wiki/The_Sentinel_(short_story)" title="The Sentinel (short story)">The Sentinel</a>". Clarke concurrently wrote the novel <i><a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(novel)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (novel)">2001: A Space Odyssey</a></i>, published soon after the film was released. The film follows a voyage to <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentient</a> computer <a href="/wiki/HAL_9000" title="HAL 9000">Hal</a> after the discovery of a <a href="/wiki/Monolith_(Space_Odyssey)" title="Monolith (Space Odyssey)">mysterious black monolith</a> affecting human evolution. The film deals with the themes of <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_evolution" title="Human evolution">human evolution</a>, technology, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a>. It is noted for its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_sci-fi" title="Hard sci-fi">scientifically accurate</a> depiction of space flight, pioneering special effects, and ambiguous imagery. It uses sound and minimal dialogue in place of traditional narrative techniques; the soundtrack consists of <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Gayane_(ballet)" title="Gayane (ballet)">Gayane Ballet Suite</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Danube" title="The Blue Danube">The Blue Danube</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Also_sprach_Zarathustra_(Strauss)" title="Also sprach Zarathustra (Strauss)">Also sprach Zarathustra</a></i>.</p>
<p>Financed and distributed by American studio <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChapmanCull2013_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChapmanCull2013-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McAleer2013_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-McAleer2013-9">[9]</a></sup> <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i> was filmed and edited almost entirely in England, using the studio facilities of the <a href="/wiki/MGM-British_Studios" title="MGM-British Studios">MGM-British Studios</a> and those of <a href="/wiki/Shepperton_Studios" title="Shepperton Studios">Shepperton Studios</a>, mostly because of the availability of much larger <a href="/wiki/Sound_stage" title="Sound stage">sound stages</a> than in the United States. Production was subcontracted to Kubrick's production company and care was taken that the film would be sufficiently British to qualify for subsidy from the <a href="/wiki/Eady_Levy" title="Eady Levy">Eady Levy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChapmanCull2013_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChapmanCull2013-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:98</sup> Having already shot his previous two films in England, Kubrick decided to settle there permanently during filming.</p>
<p>Despite initially receiving mixed reactions from critics and audiences, <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i> garnered a cult following and slowly became the highest-grossing North American film of 1968. It was nominated for four <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Awards</a>, and received one for its <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Visual_Effects" title="Academy Award for Best Visual Effects">visual effects</a>. A sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/2010_(film)" title="2010 (film)">2010</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hyams" title="Peter Hyams">Peter Hyams</a> was released in 1984.</p>
<p>Today, <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i> is widely regarded as one of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Films_considered_the_greatest_ever" title="Films considered the greatest ever">the greatest and most influential films ever made</a>. In 1991, it was deemed "culturally, historically, or aesthetically significant" by the United States <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> and selected for preservation in the <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Registry" title="National Film Registry">National Film Registry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The critics' polls in the 2002 and 2012 editions of <i><a href="/wiki/Sight_%26_Sound" title="Sight &amp; Sound">Sight &amp; Sound</a></i> magazine ranked <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i> sixth in the top ten films of all time; it also tied for second place in the directors' poll of the same magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012_poll_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-2012_poll-12">[12]</a></sup> In 2010, it was named the greatest film of all time by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Moving_Arts_Film_Journal" title="The Moving Arts Film Journal">The Moving Arts Film Journal</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Production"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Production</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plot">Plot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Plot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In an African desert millions of years ago, a tribe of ape-men is driven away from their water hole by a rival tribe. They awaken to find a <a href="/wiki/Monolith_(Space_Odyssey)" title="Monolith (Space Odyssey)">featureless black monolith</a> has appeared before them. Guided in some fashion by the monolith, they learn how to use a bone as a weapon and drive their rivals away from the water hole.</p>
<p>Millions of years later, a <a href="/wiki/Pan_American_World_Airways" title="Pan American World Airways">Pan Am</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_spacecraft_from_the_Space_Odyssey_series#Orion_III_spaceplane" title="List of spacecraft from the Space Odyssey series">space plane</a> carries Dr. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heywood_Floyd" title="Heywood Floyd">Heywood Floyd</a> to a space station orbiting Earth for a layover on his trip to <a href="/wiki/Clavius_Base" title="Clavius Base">Clavius Base</a>, a United States outpost on the moon. After a <a href="/wiki/Videophone" title="Videophone">videophone</a> call with his daughter, Floyd's Soviet scientist friend and her colleague ask about rumors of a mysterious epidemic at Clavius. Floyd declines to answer. At Clavius, Floyd heads a meeting of base personnel, apologizing for the epidemic cover story but stressing secrecy. His mission is to investigate a recently found artifact buried four million years ago. Floyd and others ride in a <a href="/wiki/List_of_spacecraft_from_the_Space_Odyssey_series#Moonbus" title="List of spacecraft from the Space Odyssey series">Moonbus</a> to the artifact, a monolith identical to the one encountered by the ape-men. Sunlight strikes the monolith and a loud high-pitched radio signal is heard.</p>
<p>Eighteen months later, the United States spacecraft <i><a href="/wiki/Discovery_One" title="Discovery One">Discovery One</a></i> is bound for <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. On board are mission pilots and scientists Dr. David Bowman and Dr. Frank Poole along with three other scientists in <a href="/wiki/Suspended_animation" title="Suspended animation">cryogenic hibernation</a>. Most of <i>Discovery's</i> operations are controlled by the ship's computer, <a href="/wiki/HAL_9000" title="HAL 9000">HAL 9000</a>, referred to by the crew as "Hal". Hal states that he is "foolproof and incapable of error". When Bowman questions Hal on the purpose of the mission, Hal responds by reporting the imminent failure of an antenna control device. The astronauts retrieve it in an EVA pod but find nothing wrong. Hal suggests reinstalling the device and letting it fail so the problem can be found. Mission Control advises the astronauts that results from their twin HAL 9000 indicate that Hal is in error. Hal insists that the problem, like previous issues ascribed to HAL series units, is due to human error. Concerned about Hal's behavior, Bowman and Poole enter an EVA pod to talk without Hal overhearing, and agree to disconnect Hal if he is proven wrong. Hal secretly follows their conversation by <a href="/wiki/Lip_reading" title="Lip reading">lip reading</a>.</p>
<p>While Poole is on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">space walk</a> outside his EVA pod attempting to replace the unit, Hal takes control of the pod, severs his oxygen hose and sets him adrift. Bowman takes another pod to attempt rescue. Meanwhile, Hal turns off the life support functions of the crewmen in suspended animation. When Bowman returns to the ship with Poole's body, Hal refuses to let him in, stating that the astronauts' plan to deactivate him jeopardizes the mission. Bowman opens the ship's emergency airlock manually, enters the ship, and proceeds to Hal's processor core. Hal tries to reassure Bowman, then pleads with him to stop, and finally expresses fear. As Bowman gradually deactivates the circuits controlling HAL's higher intellectual functions, HAL regresses to his earliest programmed memory, the song "<a href="/wiki/Daisy_Bell" title="Daisy Bell">Daisy Bell</a>", which he sings for Bowman.</p>
<p>When Bowman finally disconnects Hal, a prerecorded video message from Floyd reveals the existence of the monolith on the moon, its purpose and origin unknown. With the exception of one short but extremely powerful radio emission aimed at Jupiter, the object has been inert.</p>
<p>At Jupiter, Bowman leaves <i>Discovery One</i> in an EVA pod to investigate another monolith discovered in orbit around the planet. The pod is pulled into a vortex of colored light, and Bowman races across vast distances of space, viewing bizarre cosmological phenomena and strange landscapes of unusual colors.</p>
<p>Bowman finds himself in a bedroom appointed in the <a href="/wiki/Neoclassicism" title="Neoclassicism">neoclassical</a> style. He sees, and then becomes, older versions of himself, first standing in the bedroom, middle-aged and still in his spacesuit, then dressed in leisure attire and eating dinner, and finally as an old man lying in the bed. A monolith appears at the foot of the bed, and as Bowman reaches for it, he is transformed into a <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> enclosed in a transparent orb of light. The new being floats in space beside the Earth, gazing at it.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_and_writing">Development and writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Development and writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meeting_of_Kubrick_and_Clarke">Meeting of Kubrick and Clarke</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Meeting of Kubrick and Clarke">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kubrick_and_Arthur_Clarke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="366" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Kubrick_and_Arthur_Clarke.jpg/240px-Kubrick_and_Arthur_Clarke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Kubrick_and_Arthur_Clarke.jpg/360px-Kubrick_and_Arthur_Clarke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/94/Kubrick_and_Arthur_Clarke.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kubrick_and_Arthur_Clarke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kubrick" title="Stanley Kubrick">Stanley Kubrick</a> and Arthur C. Clarke in Kubrick's apartment office on Central Park West, New York, during the writing of <i>2001</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After completing <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Strangelove" title="Dr. Strangelove">Dr. Strangelove</a></i> (1964), director <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kubrick" title="Stanley Kubrick">Stanley Kubrick</a> became fascinated by the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and resolved to make "the proverbial good science fiction movie".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lost_Worlds_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lost_Worlds-16">[16]</a></sup> Searching for a collaborator in the <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> community, Kubrick was advised by a mutual acquaintance, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures</a> staffer Roger Caras, to talk to writer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>. Although convinced that Clarke was "a recluse, a nut who lives in a tree", Kubrick allowed Caras to cable the film proposal to Clarke, who lived in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Ceylon</a>. Clarke's cabled response stated that he was "frightfully interested in working with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%27enfant_terrible" title="L'enfant terrible">enfant terrible</a></i>", and added "what makes Kubrick think I'm a recluse?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanley_Kubrick_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanley_Kubrick-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Meeting for the first time at <a href="/wiki/Trader_Vic%27s" title="Trader Vic's">Trader Vic's</a> in New York on April 22, 1964, the two began discussing the project that would take up the next four years of their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-19">[19]</a></sup> Clarke kept a diary throughout his involvement with <i>2001</i>, excerpts of which were published in 1972 as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_Worlds_of_2001" title="The Lost Worlds of 2001">The Lost Worlds of 2001</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Search_for_source_material">Search for source material</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Search for source material">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Kubrick told Clarke he wanted to make a film about "Man's relationship to the universe",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and was, in Clarke's words, "determined to create a work of art which would arouse the emotions of wonder, awe ... even, if appropriate, terror".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-19">[19]</a></sup> Clarke offered Kubrick six of his short stories, and by May 1964, Kubrick had chosen <a href="/wiki/The_Sentinel_(short_story)" title="The Sentinel (short story)">"The Sentinel"</a> as the source material for the film. In search of more material to expand the film's plot, the two spent the rest of 1964 reading books on science and anthropology, screening science fiction films, and brainstorming ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> They spent two years transforming "The Sentinel" into a novel, and then into a script for <i>2001</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Clarke said that his short story "<a href="/wiki/Encounter_in_the_Dawn" title="Encounter in the Dawn">Encounter in the Dawn</a>" inspired the film's "Dawn Of Man" sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Kubrick and Clarke privately referred to the project as <i>How the Solar System Was Won</i> as a reference to MGM's 1962 <a href="/wiki/Cinerama" title="Cinerama">Cinerama</a> epic, <i><a href="/wiki/How_the_West_Was_Won_(film)" title="How the West Was Won (film)">How the West Was Won</a></i>. On February 23, 1965, Kubrick issued a press release announcing the title <i>Journey Beyond The Stars</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Other titles considered include <i>Universe</i>, <i>Tunnel to the Stars</i>, and <i>Planetfall</i>. In April 1965, eleven months after they began working on the project, Kubrick selected <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i>; Clarke said the title was "entirely" Kubrick's idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Intending to set the film apart from the "monsters and sex" type of science fiction films of the time, Kubrick used <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer's</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Odyssey" title="The Odyssey">The Odyssey</a></i> as inspiration for the title. Kubrick said, "[i]t occurred to us that for the Greeks the vast stretches of the sea must have had the same sort of mystery and remoteness that space has for our generation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parallel_development_of_film_and_novel">Parallel development of film and novel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Parallel development of film and novel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(novel)#Differences_from_the_film" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (novel)">Differences between the film and the novel</a></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"How much would we appreciate <i><a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa" title="Mona Lisa">La Gioconda</a></i> today if Leonardo had written at the bottom of the canvas: "This lady is smiling slightly because she has rotten teeth"—or "because she's hiding a secret from her lover"? It would shut off the viewer's appreciation and shackle him to a reality other than his own. I don't want that to happen to <i>2001</i>."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Stanley Kubrick, <i><a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">Playboy</a></i>, 1968<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Kubrick and Clarke planned to develop the <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(novel)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (novel)"><i>2001</i> novel</a> first, free of the constraints of film, and then write the screenplay. They planned the writing credits to be "Screenplay by Stanley Kubrick and Arthur C. Clarke, based on a novel by Arthur C. Clarke and Stanley Kubrick" to reflect their preeminence in their respective fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agel.2FBernstein_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agel.2FBernstein-29">[29]</a></sup> In practice, the screenplay developed in parallel to the novel, and elements were shared between both. In a 1970 interview, Kubrick said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There are a number of differences between the book and the movie. The novel, for example, attempts to explain things much more explicitly than the film does, which is inevitable in a verbal medium. The novel came about after we did a 130-page prose treatment of the film at the very outset. This initial treatment was subsequently changed in the screenplay, and the screenplay in turn was altered during the making of the film. But Arthur took all the existing material, plus an impression of some of the rushes, and wrote the novel. As a result, there's a difference between the novel and the film ... I think that the divergences between the two works are interesting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gelmis_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gelmis-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The screenplay credits were shared whereas the <i>2001</i> novel, released shortly after the film, was attributed to Clarke alone. Clarke wrote later that "the nearest approximation to the complicated truth" is that the screenplay should be credited to "Kubrick and Clarke" and the novel to "Clarke and Kubrick".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_pp.31-38_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke_pp.31-38-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Clarke and Kubrick wrote the novel and screenplay simultaneously. Clarke opted for clearer explanations of the mysterious monolith and Star Gate in the novel; Kubrick made the film more cryptic by minimising dialogue and explanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krusch_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-krusch-32">[32]</a></sup> Kubrick said the film is "basically a visual, nonverbal experience" that "hits the viewer at an inner level of consciousness, just as music does, or painting".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Depiction_of_alien_life">Depiction of alien life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Depiction of alien life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Astronomer <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> wrote in his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cosmic_Connection" title="The Cosmic Connection">The Cosmic Connection</a></i> that Clarke and Kubrick asked his opinion on how to best depict extraterrestrial intelligence. Sagan, while acknowledging Kubrick's desire to use actors to portray humanoid aliens for convenience's sake, argued that alien life forms were unlikely to bear any resemblance to terrestrial life, and that to do so would introduce "at least an element of falseness" to the film. Sagan proposed that the film suggest, rather than depict, extraterrestrial superintelligence. He attended the premiere and was "pleased to see that I had been of some help."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Kubrick hinted at the nature of the mysterious unseen alien race in <i>2001</i> by suggesting, in a 1968 interview, that given millions of years of evolution, they progressed from biological beings to "immortal machine entities", and then into "beings of pure energy and spirit"; beings with "limitless capabilities and ungraspable intelligence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Playboy_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Playboy-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stages_of_script_and_novel_development">Stages of script and novel development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Stages of script and novel development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The script went through many stages. In early 1965, when backing was secured for the film, Clarke and Kubrick still had no firm idea of what would happen to Bowman after the Star Gate sequence. Initially all of <i>Discovery</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s astronauts were to survive the journey; by October 3, Clarke and Kubrick had decided to leave Bowman the sole survivor and have him regress to infancy. By October 17, Kubrick had come up with what Clarke called a "wild idea of slightly <a href="/wiki/Fagging" title="Fagging">fag</a> robots who create a <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a> environment to put our heroes at their ease."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_pp.31-38_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke_pp.31-38-31">[31]</a></sup> HAL 9000 was originally named Athena after the <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Greek goddess of wisdom</a> and had a feminine voice and persona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_pp.31-38_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke_pp.31-38-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early drafts included a prologue containing interviews with scientists about extraterrestrial life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agelpageneeded_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agelpageneeded-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Voice-over" title="Voice-over">voice-over</a> narration (a feature in all of Kubrick's previous films),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> a stronger emphasis on the prevailing Cold War <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_terror" title="Balance of terror">balance of terror</a>, and a different and more explicitly explained breakdown for HAL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-visual-memory_a_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-visual-memory_a-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_Novel_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke_Novel-40">[40]</a></sup> Other changes include a different monolith for the "Dawn of Man" sequence, discarded when early prototypes did not photograph well; the use of Saturn as the final destination of the <i>Discovery</i> mission rather than Jupiter, discarded when the special effects team could not develop a convincing rendition of <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">Saturn's rings</a>; and the finale of the Star Child exploding nuclear weapons carried by Earth-orbiting satellites,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_Novel_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke_Novel-40">[40]</a></sup> which Kubrick discarded for its similarity to his previous film, <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Strangelove" title="Dr. Strangelove">Dr. Strangelove</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agelpageneeded_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Agelpageneeded-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_Novel_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke_Novel-40">[40]</a></sup> The finale and many of the other discarded screenplay ideas survived into Clarke's novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_Novel_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke_Novel-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Kubrick made further changes due to his desire to make the film more non-verbal, communicating at a visual and visceral level rather than through conventional narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Vincent LeBrutto writes that Clarke's novel has "strong narrative structure", while the film is a mainly visual experience where much remains symbolic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Remnants_of_early_drafts_in_final_film">Remnants of early drafts in final film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Remnants of early drafts in final film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="HAL.27s_breakdown">HAL's breakdown</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: HAL's breakdown">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HAL9000_Case.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1009" data-file-width="349" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/HAL9000_Case.svg/100px-HAL9000_Case.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/HAL9000_Case.svg/150px-HAL9000_Case.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/HAL9000_Case.svg/200px-HAL9000_Case.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HAL9000_Case.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
HAL9000 interface</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the film leaves it mysterious, early script drafts made clear that HAL's breakdown is triggered by authorities on Earth who order him to withhold information from the astronauts about the purpose of the mission (this is also explained in the film's sequel <i><a href="/wiki/2010_(film)" title="2010 (film)">2010</a></i>). Frederick Ordway, Kubrick's science advisor and technical consultant, stated that in an earlier script Poole tells HAL there is "... something about this mission that we weren't told. Something the rest of the crew knows and that you know. We would like to know whether this is true", to which HAL responds: "I'm sorry, Frank, but I don't think I can answer that question without knowing everything that all of you know."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-visual-memory_a_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-visual-memory_a-38">[38]</a></sup> HAL then falsely predicts a failure of the hardware maintaining radio contact with Earth (the source of HAL's difficult orders) during the broadcast of Frank Poole's birthday greetings from his parents.</p>
<p>The final script removed this explanation, but it is hinted at when HAL asks David Bowman if Bowman is bothered by the "oddities" and "tight security" surrounding the mission. After Bowman concludes that HAL is dutifully drawing up the "crew psychology report", the computer makes his false prediction of hardware failure. Another hint occurs at the moment of HAL's deactivation when a video reveals the purpose of the mission.</p>
<p>In an interview with Joseph Gelmis in 1969, Kubrick stated that HAL "had an acute emotional crisis because he could not accept evidence of his own fallibility".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_nature_of_orbiting_satellites">Military nature of orbiting satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Military nature of orbiting satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Interpretations_of_2001:_A_Space_Odyssey#Military_Nature_of_Orbiting_Satellites" title="Interpretations of 2001: A Space Odyssey">Interpretations of 2001: A Space Odyssey § Military Nature of Orbiting Satellites</a></div>
<p>Kubrick originally planned a voice-over to reveal that the satellites seen after the prologue are nuclear weapons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and that the Star Child would detonate the weapons at the end of the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> However, he decided this would create associations with his previous film <i>Dr. Strangelove,</i> and decided not to make it so obvious that they were "war machines".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bizony_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bizony-46">[46]</a></sup> A few weeks before the release of the film, the U.S. and Soviet governments had agreed not to put any nuclear <a href="/wiki/Militarisation_of_space" title="Militarisation of space">weapons into outer space</a>.</p>
<p>In a book he wrote with Kubrick's assistance, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Walker_(critic)" title="Alexander Walker (critic)">Alexander Walker</a> states that Kubrick eventually decided that as nuclear weapons the bombs had "no place at all in the film's thematic development", now being an "orbiting red herring" which would "merely have raised irrelevant questions to suggest this as a reality of the twenty-first century".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Kubrick scholar <a href="/wiki/Michel_Ciment" title="Michel Ciment">Michel Ciment</a>, discussing Kubrick's attitude toward human aggression and instinct, observes: "The bone cast into the air by the ape (now become a man) is transformed at the other extreme of civilization, by one of those abrupt ellipses characteristic of the director, into a spacecraft on its way to the moon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In contrast to Ciment's reading of a cut to a serene "other extreme of civilization", science fiction novelist <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._Sawyer" title="Robert J. Sawyer">Robert Sawyer</a>, speaking in the Canadian documentary <i>2001 and Beyond</i>, saw it as a cut from a bone to a nuclear weapons platform, explaining that "what we see is not how far we've leaped ahead, what we see is that today, '2001', and four million years ago on the African veldt, it's exactly the same—the power of mankind is the power of its weapons. It's a continuation, not a discontinuity in that jump."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beyond_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-beyond-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialogue">Dialogue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Dialogue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The film contains no dialogue for the first and last 20 minutes or so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roger_Ebert_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roger_Ebert-50">[50]</a></sup> By the time shooting began, Kubrick had removed much of the dialogue and narration; what remains is notable for its banality (making the computer Hal seem to have more emotion than the humans) juxtaposed with epic space scenes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first scenes of dialogue are Floyd's encounters on the space station: chit-chat with the colleague who greets him, his telephone call to his daughter, and the friendly but strained encounter with Soviet scientists. Later, en route to the monolith, Floyd engages in trite exchanges with his staff while a spectacular journey by Earth-light across the Lunar surface is shown. Hal is the only character who expresses anxiety, as well as feelings of pride and bewilderment.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visualizing_space_and_space_travel">Visualizing space and space travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Visualizing space and space travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Kubrick's decision to avoid the fanciful portrayals of space in standard popular science fiction films of the time led him to seek a more realistic and scientifically accurate visualization of space travel. Illustrators such as <a href="/wiki/Chesley_Bonestell" title="Chesley Bonestell">Chesley Bonestell</a>, Roy Carnon, and Richard McKenna were hired to produce concept drawings, sketches and paintings of the space technology seen in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scienceandfilm.org_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-scienceandfilm.org-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Two educational films that came out previously, the 1960 <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Board_of_Canada" title="National Film Board of Canada">National Film Board of Canada</a> animated short documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Universe_(1960_film)" title="Universe (1960 film)">Universe</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/1964_New_York_World%27s_Fair" title="1964 New York World's Fair">1964 New York World's Fair</a> movie <i><a href="/wiki/To_the_Moon_and_Beyond" title="To the Moon and Beyond">To the Moon and Beyond</a></i> were very influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scienceandfilm.org_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-scienceandfilm.org-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to biographer Vincent Lobrutto, <i>Universe</i> was a visual inspiration to Kubrick. The 29 minute film, which had also proved popular at <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> for its realistic portrayal of outer space, achieved "the standard of dynamic visionary realism that he was looking for." Wally Gentleman, one of the special effects artists on <i>Universe</i>, worked briefly on <i>2001</i>. Kubrick also asked <i>Universe</i> co-director <a href="/wiki/Colin_Low_(filmmaker)" title="Colin Low (filmmaker)">Colin Low</a> about animation camerawork, with Low recommending British mathematician <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Salt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Brian Salt (page does not exist)">Brian Salt</a>, with whom Low and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Kroitor" title="Roman Kroitor">Roman Kroitor</a> had previously worked on the 1957 still animation documentary, <i><a href="/wiki/City_of_Gold_(1957_film)" title="City of Gold (1957 film)">City of Gold</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-55">[55]</a></sup> <i>Universe</i> would have one more influence on <i>2001</i> when its narrator, actor <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Rain" title="Douglas Rain">Douglas Rain</a>, relatively unknown outside Canada, was cast as the voice of HAL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lacey_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lacey-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>After pre-production had begun Kubrick saw the 1964 World's Fair film <i>To the Moon and Beyond</i>, a film shown in the Transportation and Travel building that had been filmed in Cinerama 360 and was being shown in the "Moon Dome". He ended up hiring the company that produced it, Graphic Films Corporation, which had been making films for NASA, US Air Force, and various aerospace clients, as a design consultant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scienceandfilm.org_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-scienceandfilm.org-52">[52]</a></sup> Graphic Films' <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Con_Pederson&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Con Pederson (page does not exist)">Con Pederson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lester_Novros" title="Lester Novros">Lester Novros</a>, and background artist <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Trumbull" title="Douglas Trumbull">Douglas Trumbull</a> would air-mail research based concept sketches and notes covering the mechanics and physics of space travel and go on to create <a href="/wiki/Storyboard" title="Storyboard">storyboards</a> for the space flight sequences seen in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scienceandfilm.org_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-scienceandfilm.org-52">[52]</a></sup> Trumbull would go on to become a special effects supervisor on <i>2001</i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Filming">Filming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Filming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Principal_photography" title="Principal photography">Principal photography</a> began December 29, 1965, in Stage H at <a href="/wiki/Shepperton_Studios" title="Shepperton Studios">Shepperton Studios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shepperton" title="Shepperton">Shepperton</a>, England. The studio was chosen because it could house the 60-by-120-by-60-foot (18 m × 37 m × 18 m) pit for the <a href="/wiki/Tycho_(crater)" title="Tycho (crater)">Tycho</a> crater excavation scene, the first to be shot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gedult_calender_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-gedult_calender-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The production moved in January 1966 to the smaller <a href="/wiki/MGM-British_Studios" title="MGM-British Studios">MGM-British Studios</a> in <a href="/wiki/Borehamwood" title="Borehamwood">Borehamwood</a>, where the live action and special effects filming was done, starting with the scenes involving Floyd on the Orion spaceplane;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> it was described as a "huge throbbing nerve center ... with much the same frenetic atmosphere as a Cape Kennedy blockhouse during the final stages of Countdown."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filming_2001_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-filming_2001-60">[60]</a></sup> The only scene not filmed in a studio—and the last live-action scene shot for the film—was the skull-smashing sequence, in which Moonwatcher (Richter) wields his new-found bone "weapon-tool" against a pile of nearby animal bones. A small elevated platform was built in a field near the studio so that the camera could shoot upward with the sky as background, avoiding cars and trucks passing by in the distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richter_2002_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richter_2002-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Filming of actors was completed in September 1967,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and from June 1966 until March 1968 Kubrick spent most of his time working on the 205 special effects shots in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gelmis_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gelmis-30">[30]</a></sup> The director ordered the special effects technicians on <i>2001</i> to use the painstaking process of creating all visual effects seen in the film "in camera", avoiding degraded picture quality from the use of <a href="/wiki/Chroma_key" title="Chroma key">blue screen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matte_(filmmaking)" title="Matte (filmmaking)">traveling matte</a> techniques. Although this technique, known as "held takes", resulted in a much better image, it meant exposed film would be stored for long periods of time between shots, sometimes as long as a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In March 1968, Kubrick finished the 'pre-premiere' editing of the film, making his final cuts just days before the film's general release in April 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gelmis_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gelmis-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The film was announced in 1965 as a "<a href="/wiki/Cinerama" title="Cinerama">Cinerama</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> film and was photographed in <a href="/wiki/Super_Panavision_70" title="Super Panavision 70">Super Panavision 70</a> (which uses a 65 mm negative combined with spherical lenses to create an aspect ratio of 2.20:1). It would eventually be released in a limited "<a href="/wiki/Roadshow_theatrical_release" title="Roadshow theatrical release">road-show</a>" <a href="/wiki/Cinerama" title="Cinerama">Cinerama</a> version, then in 70mm and 35mm versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Color processing and 35 mm release prints were done using <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">Technicolor</a>'s dye transfer process. The 70 mm prints were made by MGM Laboratories, Inc. on <a href="/wiki/Metrocolor" title="Metrocolor">Metrocolor</a>. The production was $4.5 million over the initial $6.0 million budget, and sixteen months behind schedule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gedult_calender_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-gedult_calender-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the opening sequence involving tribes of apes, professional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mime" title="Mime">mime</a> Daniel Richter in addition to playing the lead ape was also responsible for choreographing the movements of the other man-apes, who were mostly portrayed by his standing mime troupe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richter_2002_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richter_2002-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Set_design_and_furnishings">Set design and furnishings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Set design and furnishings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Kubrick involved himself in every aspect of production, even choosing the fabric for his actors' costumes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and selecting notable pieces of contemporary furniture for use in the film. When Floyd exits the Space Station V elevator, he is greeted by an attendant seated behind a slightly modified <a href="/wiki/George_Nelson_(designer)" title="George Nelson (designer)">George Nelson</a> Action Office desk from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herman_Miller_(office_equipment)" title="Herman Miller (office equipment)">Herman Miller</a>'s 1964 "<a href="/wiki/Action_Office" title="Action Office">Action Office</a>" series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> First introduced in 1968, the Action Office-style "<a href="/wiki/Cubicle" title="Cubicle">cubicle</a>" would eventually occupy 70 percent of office space by the mid-2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Danish designer <a href="/wiki/Arne_Jacobsen" title="Arne Jacobsen">Arne Jacobsen</a> designed the cutlery used by the <i>Discovery</i> astronauts in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other examples of modern furniture in the film are the bright red <a href="/wiki/Djinn_chair" title="Djinn chair">Djinn chairs</a> seen prominently throughout the space station<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phil_Patton_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phil_Patton-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fiell2005_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fiell2005-76">[76]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Eero_Saarinen" title="Eero Saarinen">Eero Saarinen</a>'s 1956 pedestal tables. <a href="/wiki/Olivier_Mourgue" title="Olivier Mourgue">Olivier Mourgue</a>, designer of the Djinn chair, has used the connection to <i>2001</i> in his advertising; a frame from the film's space station sequence and three production stills appear on the homepage of Mourgue's website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Shortly before Kubrick's death, film critic Alexander Walker informed Kubrick of Mourgue's use of the film, joking to him "You're keeping the price up".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Commenting on their use in the film, Walker writes:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Everyone recalls one early sequence in the film, the space hotel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> primarily because the custom-made Olivier Mourgue furnishings, those foam-filled sofas, undulant and serpentine, are covered in scarlet fabric and are the first stabs of color one sees. They resemble Rorschach "blots" against the pristine purity of the rest of the lobby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Detailed instructions in relatively small print for various technological devices appear at several points in the film, the most visible of which are the lengthy instructions for the zero-gravity toilet on the Aries Moon shuttle. Similar detailed instructions for replacing the explosive bolts also appear on the hatches of the E.V.A. pods, most visibly in closeup just before Bowman's pod leaves the ship to rescue Frank Poole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The film features an extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Eurostile" title="Eurostile">Eurostile Bold Extended</a>, <a href="/wiki/Futura_(typeface)" title="Futura (typeface)">Futura</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sans_serif" title="Sans serif">sans serif</a> <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">typefaces</a> as design elements of the <i>2001</i> world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Computer displays show high resolution fonts, color and graphics—far in advance of computers in the 1960s when the film was made.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_effects">Special effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Special effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Technologies_in_2001:_A_Space_Odyssey" title="Technologies in 2001: A Space Odyssey">Technologies in 2001: A Space Odyssey</a></div>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">

<td style="width:1px"></td>

<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:2001_space_travel.ogv" title="File:2001 space travel.ogv">Special effects in <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i></a></div>
<div>
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:512px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/2001_space_travel.ogv/512px--2001_space_travel.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:512px;height:288px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="116.34956666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="2001_space_travel.ogv" data-mwprovider="local"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fa/2001_space_travel.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 512 × 288 (486 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="512" data-height="288" data-bandwidth="485520" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/f/fa/2001_space_travel.ogv/2001_space_travel.ogv.160p.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth Ogg video (160P)" data-shorttitle="Ogg 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.ogv" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="157904" data-framerate="15"/&gt;&lt;track src="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=TimedText:2001_space_travel.ogv.en.srt&amp;amp;action=raw&amp;amp;ctype=text%2Fx-srt" kind="subtitles" type="text/x-srt" srclang="en" label="English (en) subtitles" data-mwtitle="TimedText:2001_space_travel.ogv.en.srt" data-mwprovider="local" data-dir="ltr"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:2001 space travel.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/2001_space_travel.ogv/220px--2001_space_travel.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fa/2001_space_travel.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>

<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Front_projection">Front projection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Front projection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i>2001</i> pioneered the use of <a href="/wiki/Front_projection_effect" title="Front projection effect">front projection</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retroreflective" title="Retroreflective">retroreflective</a> <a href="/wiki/Matte_(filmmaking)" title="Matte (filmmaking)">matting</a>. Kubrick used the technique to produce the backdrops in the Africa scenes and the scene when astronauts walk on the moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>The technique consisted of a separate scenery projector set at a right-angle to the camera, and a half-silvered mirror placed at an angle in front that reflected the projected image forward in line with the camera lens onto a backdrop made of retroreflective material. The reflective directional screen behind the actors could reflect light from the projected image a hundred times more efficiently than the foreground subject did. The lighting of the foreground subject had to be balanced with the image from the screen, making the image from the scenery projector on the subject too faint to record. The exception was the eyes of the <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopard</a> in the "Dawn of Man" sequence, which glowed orange from the projector illumination. Kubrick described this as "a happy accident".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herb_A._Lightman_page2_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herb_A._Lightman_page2-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Front projection had been used in smaller settings before <i>2001,</i> mostly for still photography or television production, using small still images and projectors. The expansive backdrops for the African scenes required a screen 40 feet (12 m) tall and 110 feet (34 m) wide, far larger than had been used before. When the reflective material was applied to the backdrop in 100-foot (30 m) strips, variations at the seams of the strips led to visual artifacts; to solve this, the crew tore the material into smaller chunks and applied them in a random "camouflage" pattern on the backdrop. The existing projectors using 4-×-5-inch (10 × 13 cm) transparencies resulted in grainy images when projected that large, so the crew worked with MGM's special effects supervisor <a href="/wiki/Tom_Howard_(special_effects)" title="Tom Howard (special effects)">Tom Howard</a> to build a custom projector using 8-×-10-inch (20 × 25 cm) transparencies, which required the largest water-cooled arc lamp available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herb_A._Lightman_page2_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herb_A._Lightman_page2-84">[84]</a></sup> The technique was used widely in the film industry afterwards until it was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Chroma_key" title="Chroma key">blue/green screen</a> systems in the 1990s.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Models">Models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stanley_Kubrick_in_EYE_(7527338298).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2664" data-file-width="4000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Stanley_Kubrick_in_EYE_%287527338298%29.jpg/220px-Stanley_Kubrick_in_EYE_%287527338298%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Stanley_Kubrick_in_EYE_%287527338298%29.jpg/330px-Stanley_Kubrick_in_EYE_%287527338298%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Stanley_Kubrick_in_EYE_%287527338298%29.jpg/440px-Stanley_Kubrick_in_EYE_%287527338298%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stanley_Kubrick_in_EYE_(7527338298).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Modern replica of the <i>Discovery One</i> spaceship model</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To heighten the reality of the film very intricate models of the various spacecraft and locations were built. Their sizes ranged from about two-foot long models of satellites and the Aries translunar shuttle up to a 55-foot long <i>Discovery One</i> spacecraft. "In-camera" techniques were again used as much as possible to combine models and background shots together to prevent degradation of the image through continual duplicating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online_excerpt_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-online_excerpt-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In shots where there was no perspective change, still shots of the models were photographed and positive paper prints were made. The image of the model was cut out of the photographic print and mounted on glass and filmed on an <a href="/wiki/Animation_stand" title="Animation stand">animation stand</a>. The undeveloped film was re-wound to film the star background with the silhouette of the model photograph acting as a <a href="/wiki/Matte_(filmmaking)" title="Matte (filmmaking)">matte</a> to block out where the spaceship image was.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online_excerpt_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-online_excerpt-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shots where the spacecraft had parts in motion or the perspective changed were shot by directly filming the model. For most shots the model was stationary and camera was driven along a track on a special mount, the motor of which was mechanically linked to the camera motor—making it possible to repeat camera moves and match speeds exactly. Elements of the scene were recorded on same piece of film in separate passes to combine the lit model, stars, planets, or other spacecraft in the same shot. In moving shots of the long <i>Discovery One</i> spacecraft, in order to keep the entire model in focus, multiple passes had to be made with the lighting on it blocked out section by section. In each pass the camera would be focused on the one lit section.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Matte_(filmmaking)" title="Matte (filmmaking)">matting</a> techniques were tried to block out the stars behind the models, with film makers sometimes resorting to hand tracing frame by frame around the image of the spacecraft (<a href="/wiki/Rotoscoping" title="Rotoscoping">rotoscoping</a>) to create the matte.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online_excerpt_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-online_excerpt-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bizony_2001.2C_pp._113.E2.80.93117_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bizony_2001.2C_pp._113.E2.80.93117-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some shots required exposing the film again to record previously filmed live action shots of the people appearing in the windows the spacecraft or structures, achieved by mounting projection devices inside the model or, when two dimensional photographs were used, projecting from the backside through a hole cut in the photograph.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online_excerpt_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-online_excerpt-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>All of the shots required multiple takes so that some film could be developed and printed to check exposure, density, alignment of elements, and to supply footage used in further elements such as matting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online_excerpt_85-4"><a href="#cite_note-online_excerpt-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bizony_2001.2C_pp._113.E2.80.93117_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bizony_2001.2C_pp._113.E2.80.93117-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotating_sets">Rotating sets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Rotating sets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2001_CENTRIFUGE_SET.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="298" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/2001_CENTRIFUGE_SET.jpg/220px-2001_CENTRIFUGE_SET.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f2/2001_CENTRIFUGE_SET.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2001_CENTRIFUGE_SET.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The "centrifuge" set used for filming scenes depicting interior of the spaceship Discovery</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For spacecraft interior shots, ostensibly containing a giant centrifuge that produces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_gravity" title="Rotational gravity">artificial gravity</a>, Kubrick had a 30-short-ton (27 t) rotating "ferris wheel" built by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vickers-Armstrong" title="Vickers-Armstrong">Vickers-Armstrong</a> Engineering Group at a cost of $750,000. The set was 38 feet (12 m) in diameter and 10 feet (3.0 m) wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Various scenes in the <i>Discovery</i> centrifuge were shot by securing set pieces within the wheel, then rotating it while the actor walked or ran in sync with its motion, keeping him at the bottom of the wheel as it turned. The camera could be fixed to the inside of the rotating wheel to show the actor walking completely "around" the set, or mounted in such a way that the wheel rotated independently of the stationary camera, as in the jogging scene where the camera appears to alternately precede and follow the running actor. The shots where the actors appear on opposite sides of the wheel required one of the actors to be strapped securely into place at the "top" of the wheel as it moved to allow the other actor to walk to the "bottom" of the wheel to join him. The most notable case is when Bowman enters the centrifuge from the central hub on a ladder, and joins Poole, who is eating on the other side of the centrifuge. This required Gary Lockwood to be strapped into a seat while Keir Dullea walked toward him from the opposite side of the wheel as it turned with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another rotating set appeared in an earlier sequence on board the Aries translunar shuttle. A stewardess is shown preparing in-flight meals, then carrying them into a circular walkway. Attached to the set as it rotates 180 degrees, the camera's point of view remains constant, and she appears to walk up the "side" of the circular walkway, and steps, now in an "upside-down" orientation, into a connecting hallway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Zero_gravity_effects">Zero gravity effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Zero gravity effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The realistic-looking effects of the astronauts floating weightless in space and inside the spacecraft were accomplished by suspending the actors from wires attached to the top of the set, and placing the camera underneath them. The actors' bodies blocked the camera's view of the suspension wires, creating a very believable appearance of floating. For the shot of Poole floating into the pod's arms during Bowman's rescue attempt, a stuntman replaced a dummy on the wire to realistically portray the movements of an unconscious human, and was shot in slow motion to enhance the illusion of drifting through space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The scene showing Bowman entering the emergency airlock from the E.V.A. pod was done in a similar way: an off-camera stagehand, standing on a platform, held the wire suspending Dullea above the camera positioned at the bottom of the vertically configured airlock. At the proper moment, the stagehand first loosened his grip on the wire, causing Dullea to fall toward the camera, then, while holding the wire firmly, he jumped off the platform, causing Dullea to ascend back up toward the hatch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2001:A_Space_Odyssey_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-2001:A_Space_Odyssey-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Star_Gate_sequence">Star Gate sequence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Star Gate sequence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Star_Gate.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="141" data-file-width="300" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/89/Star_Gate.JPG/220px-Star_Gate.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/89/Star_Gate.JPG 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Star_Gate.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The "Star Gate" sequence was one of many ground-breaking visual effects. It was primarily for these that Stanley Kubrick won his only personal <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The colored lights in the Star Gate sequence were accomplished by <a href="/wiki/Slit-scan_photography" title="Slit-scan photography">slit-scan photography</a> of thousands of high-contrast images on film, including <a href="/wiki/Op_art" title="Op art">Op art</a> paintings, architectural drawings, <a href="/wiki/Moir%C3%A9_pattern" title="Moiré pattern">Moiré patterns</a>, printed circuits, and electron-microscope photographs of molecular and crystal structures. Known to staff as "Manhattan Project", the shots of various nebula-like phenomena, including the expanding star field, were colored paints and chemicals swirling in a pool-like device known as a cloud tank, shot in slow-motion in a dark room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The live-action landscape shots in the 'Star Gate' sequence were filmed in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebridean islands</a>, the mountains of northern Scotland, and <a href="/wiki/Monument_Valley" title="Monument Valley">Monument Valley</a>. The coloring and negative-image effects were achieved by the use of different color filters in the process of making duplicate negatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(soundtrack)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (soundtrack)">2001: A Space Odyssey (soundtrack)</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(score)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (score)">2001: A Space Odyssey (score)</a></div>
<p>From very early in production, Kubrick decided that he wanted the film to be a primarily nonverbal experience<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> that did not rely on the traditional techniques of <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narrative</a> cinema, and in which music would play a vital role in evoking particular moods. About half the music in the film appears either before the first line of dialogue or after the final line. Almost no music is heard during any scenes with dialogue.</p>
<p>The film is notable for its innovative use of <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a> taken from existing commercial recordings. Most feature films then and now are typically accompanied by elaborate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_scores" title="Film scores">film scores</a> or songs written specially for them by professional composers. In the early stages of production, Kubrick had actually commissioned a <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(score)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (score)">score for <i>2001</i></a> from Hollywood composer <a href="/wiki/Alex_North" title="Alex North">Alex North</a>, who had written the score for <i><a href="/wiki/Spartacus_(film)" title="Spartacus (film)">Spartacus</a></i> and also worked on <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Strangelove" title="Dr. Strangelove">Dr. Strangelove</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> However, during postproduction, Kubrick chose to abandon North's music in favor of the now-familiar classical pieces he had earlier chosen as "guide pieces" for the soundtrack. North did not know of the abandonment of the score until after he saw the film's premiere screening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LoBrutto_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-LoBrutto-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also engaged to score the film was composer <a href="/wiki/Frank_Cordell" title="Frank Cordell">Frank Cordell</a>. Cordell stated in interviews that the score would primarily consist of arrangements of <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">Gustav Mahler</a> works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> This score remains unreleased. Like North's score, Cordell's work was recorded at the now demolished Anvil, Denham studios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>2001</i> is particularly remembered for using pieces of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Strauss_II" title="Johann Strauss II">Johann Strauss II</a>'s best-known <a href="/wiki/Waltz" title="Waltz">waltz</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Danube" title="The Blue Danube">The Blue Danube</a></i>, during the extended space-station docking and Lunar landing sequences. This is the result of the association that Kubrick made between the spinning motion of the <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a> and the dancers of <a href="/wiki/Waltz" title="Waltz">waltzes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> It also makes use of the opening from the <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">Richard Strauss</a> tone poem <i><a href="/wiki/Also_sprach_Zarathustra_(Strauss)" title="Also sprach Zarathustra (Strauss)">Also sprach Zarathustra</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> performed by the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Philharmonic" title="Vienna Philharmonic">Vienna Philharmonic</a> conducted by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_von_Karajan" title="Herbert von Karajan">Herbert von Karajan</a>. The use of Strauss's <i>Zarathustra</i> may be a reference to the theme of mankind's eventual replacement by supermen (<a href="/wiki/%C3%9Cbermensch" title="Übermensch">Übermensch</a>) in Nietzsche's work <i><a href="/wiki/Thus_Spoke_Zarathustra" title="Thus Spoke Zarathustra">Thus Spoke Zarathustra</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krusch_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-krusch-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> <i>Gayane's Adagio</i> from <a href="/wiki/Aram_Khachaturian" title="Aram Khachaturian">Aram Khachaturian</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Gayane_(ballet)" title="Gayane (ballet)">Gayane</a></i> ballet suite is heard during the sections that introduce Bowman and Poole aboard the <i>Discovery,</i> conveying a somewhat lonely and mournful quality.</p>
<p>In addition to the majestic yet fairly traditional compositions by the two Strausses and Khachaturian, Kubrick used four highly modernistic compositions by <a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_Ligeti" title="György Ligeti">György Ligeti</a> that employ <a href="/wiki/Micropolyphony" title="Micropolyphony">micropolyphony</a>, the use of sustained dissonant chords that shift slowly. This technique was pioneered in <i><a href="/wiki/Atmosph%C3%A8res" title="Atmosphères">Atmosphères</a></i>, the only Ligeti piece heard in its entirety in the film. Ligeti admired Kubrick's film but, in addition to being irritated by Kubrick's failure to obtain permission directly from him, he was offended that his music was used in a film soundtrack shared by composers Johann Strauss II and Richard Strauss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Other music used is Ligeti's <i><a href="/wiki/Lux_Aeterna_(Ligeti)" title="Lux Aeterna (Ligeti)">Lux Aeterna</a></i>, the second movement of his <i>Requiem</i> and an electronically altered form of his <i>Aventures</i>, the last of which was also used without Ligeti's permission and is not listed in the film's credits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hal's version of the popular song "<a href="/wiki/Daisy_Bell" title="Daisy Bell">Daisy Bell</a>" (referred to by Hal as "Daisy" in the film) was inspired by a computer-synthesized arrangement by <a href="/wiki/Max_Mathews" title="Max Mathews">Max Mathews</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a> had heard in 1962 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bell_Laboratories" title="Bell Laboratories">Bell Laboratories</a> Murray Hill facility when he was, coincidentally, visiting friend and colleague <a href="/wiki/John_R._Pierce" title="John R. Pierce">John R. Pierce</a>. At that time, a <a href="/wiki/Speech_synthesis" title="Speech synthesis">speech synthesis</a> demonstration was being performed by physicist <a href="/wiki/John_Larry_Kelly,_Jr." title="John Larry Kelly, Jr.">John Larry Kelly, Jr.</a>, by using an <a href="/wiki/IBM_704" title="IBM 704">IBM 704</a> computer to synthesize speech. Kelly's voice recorder synthesizer <i><a href="/wiki/Vocoder" title="Vocoder">vocoder</a></i> recreated the song "Daisy Bell" ("Bicycle Built For Two"); Max Mathews provided the musical accompaniment. Arthur C. Clarke was so impressed that he later used it in the screenplay and novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bell_labs_Hal_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-bell_labs_Hal-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many non-English language versions of the film do not use the song "Daisy". In the French soundtrack, Hal sings the French <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk</a> song "<a href="/wiki/Au_clair_de_la_lune" title="Au clair de la lune">Au clair de la lune</a>" while being disconnected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> In the German version, Hal sings the children's song "<a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4nschen_klein" title="Hänschen klein">Hänschen klein</a>" ("Little Johnny"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> and in the Italian version Hal sings "Giro giro tondo" (Ring a Ring o' Roses).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>A recording of British light music composer <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Torch" title="Sidney Torch">Sidney Torch</a>'s "Off Beat Moods Part 1" was chosen by Kubrick as the theme for the fictitious BBC news programme "The World Tonight" seen aboard the Discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 25, 2010, a version of the film specially remastered by Warner Bros, without the music soundtrack, opened the three hundred and fiftieth anniversary celebrations of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> at <a href="/wiki/Southbank_Centre" title="Southbank Centre">Southbank Centre</a> in cooperation with the <a href="/wiki/British_Film_Institute" title="British Film Institute">British Film Institute</a>. The score was played live by the <a href="/wiki/Philharmonia_Orchestra" title="Philharmonia Orchestra">Philharmonia Orchestra</a> and Choir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> This has become a recurring event at the Southbank Centre's Royal Festival Hall, with repeat performances in 2011 and on October 2, 2016. These later two performances were played by the London Philharmonic Orchestra and sung by the Philharmonia Choir, the latter as part of a more general programme of similar events entitled "Film Scores Live." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 14, 2013, a repeat presentation of the film accompanied by live orchestra and choir was performed at <a href="/wiki/Symphony_Hall,_Birmingham" title="Symphony Hall, Birmingham">Symphony Hall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>, again accompanied by the <a href="/wiki/Philharmonia_Orchestra" title="Philharmonia Orchestra">Philharmonia Orchestra</a> conducted by Benjamin Wallfisch together with the choir <a href="/wiki/Ex_Cathedra" title="Ex Cathedra">Ex Cathedra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>A presentation of the film accompanied by live orchestra and choir premiered in the United States on August 18, 2015 at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Hollywood_Bowl" title="The Hollywood Bowl">The Hollywood Bowl</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hollywood,_California" title="Hollywood, California">Hollywood, California</a>, accompanied by the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Philharmonic" title="Los Angeles Philharmonic">Los Angeles Philharmonic</a> conducted by Brad Lubman together with the choir <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Master_Chorale" title="Los Angeles Master Chorale">Los Angeles Master Chorale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
