<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Editing_and_deleted_scenes">Editing and deleted scenes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Editing and deleted scenes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MatchCut.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="510" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/45/MatchCut.JPG/200px-MatchCut.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/45/MatchCut.JPG/301px-MatchCut.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/45/MatchCut.JPG/401px-MatchCut.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MatchCut.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A bone-club and a satellite juxtaposed via match cut</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>2001</i> contains a famous example of a <a href="/wiki/Match_cut" title="Match cut">match cut</a>, a type of cut in which two shots are matched by action or subject matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dictionary_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-dictionary-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> After an ape discovers the use of bones as tools, he throws one triumphantly into the air; as the bone spins in the air, the film cuts to an orbiting satellite, marking the end of the prologue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The match cut draws a connection between the two objects as exemplars of primitive and advanced tools respectively, and demonstrates humanity's technological progress since the time of the apes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filmjournal_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-filmjournal-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>A version of the film edited before it was publicly screened<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-118">[118]</a></sup> included a painting class on the lunar base that included Kubrick's daughters, additional scenes of life on the base, and Floyd buying a <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">bush baby</a> from a department store via videophone for his daughter. A ten-minute black-and-white opening sequence featuring interviews with actual scientists, including <a href="/wiki/Freeman_Dyson" title="Freeman Dyson">Freeman Dyson</a> discussing off-Earth life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> was removed after an early screening for MGM executives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-visual-memory_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-visual-memory-120">[120]</a></sup> The text survives in the book <i>The Making of Kubrick's 2001</i> by Jerome Agel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Kubrick removed a further 19 minutes of footage following the world premier on April 2, 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-118">[118]</a></sup> These included scenes revealing details about life on <i>Discovery</i>: additional space walks, astronaut Bowman retrieving a spare part from an octagonal corridor, elements from the Poole murder sequence including space-walk preparation and Hal turning off radio contact with Poole, and a close-up of Bowman picking up a slipper during his walk in the alien room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Agel describes the cut scenes as comprising "Dawn of Man, Orion, Poole exercising in the centrifuge, and Poole's pod exiting from <i>Discovery</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Kubrick's rationale for editing the film was to tighten the narrative. Reviews<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (October 2016)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> suggested the film suffered from its departure from traditional cinematic storytelling. Regarding the cuts, Kubrick stated, "I didn't believe that the trims made a critical difference. ... The people who like it, like it no matter what its length, and the same holds true for the people who hate it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-visual-memory_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-visual-memory-120">[120]</a></sup> As was typical of most films of the era released both as a "<a href="/wiki/Roadshow_theatrical_release" title="Roadshow theatrical release">roadshow</a>" (in Cinerama format in the case of <i>2001</i>) and general release (in 70-millimetre in the case of <i>2001</i>), the entrance music, intermission music (and intermission altogether), and postcredit exit music were cut from most prints of the latter version, although these have been restored to most DVD releases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Kubrick's brother-in-law <a href="/wiki/Jan_Harlan" title="Jan Harlan">Jan Harlan</a>, the director was adamant the trims were never to be seen, and that he burned the negatives, which he had kept in his garage, shortly before his death. This was confirmed by former Kubrick assistant <a href="/wiki/Leon_Vitali" title="Leon Vitali">Leon Vitali</a>: "I'll tell you right now, okay, on <i>Clockwork Orange</i>, <i>The Shining</i>, <i>Barry Lyndon</i>, some little parts of <i>2001</i>, we had thousands of cans of negative outtakes and print, which we had stored in an area at his house where we worked out of, which he personally supervised the loading of it to a truck and then I went down to a big industrial waste lot and burned it. That's what he wanted."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2010, Douglas Trumbull announced that <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> had located seventeen minutes of lost footage from the post-premiere cuts, "perfectly preserved", in a Kansas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_mine" title="Salt mine">salt mine</a> vault.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgel1970170_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgel1970170-2">[2]</a></sup> No plans have been announced for the footage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Soundtrack">Soundtrack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Soundtrack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The initial MGM soundtrack album release contained none of the material from the altered and uncredited rendition of Ligeti's "Aventures", used a different recording of "Also sprach Zarathustra" from that heard in the film, this time performed by the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Philharmonic" title="Berlin Philharmonic">Berlin Philharmonic</a> conducted by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_von_Karajan" title="Herbert von Karajan">Herbert von Karajan</a>, and a longer excerpt of "Lux aeterna" than that in the film.</p>
<p>In 1996, Turner Entertainment/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhino_Records" title="Rhino Records">Rhino Records</a> released a new soundtrack on CD which included the material from "Aventures" and restored the version of "Zarathustra" used in the film, and used the shorter version of "Lux aeterna" from the film. As additional "bonus tracks" at the end, this CD includes the versions of "Zarathustra" and "Lux aeterna" on the old MGM soundtrack, an <i>unaltered</i> performance of "Aventures", and a nine-minute compilation of all of Hal's dialogue from the film.</p>
<p>North's unused music had its first public appearance in Telarc's issue of the main theme on <i>Hollywood's Greatest Hits, Vol. 2</i>, a compilation album by <a href="/wiki/Erich_Kunzel" title="Erich Kunzel">Erich Kunzel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Pops_Orchestra" title="Cincinnati Pops Orchestra">Cincinnati Pops Orchestra</a>. All the music North originally wrote was recorded commercially by North's friend and colleague <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Goldsmith" title="Jerry Goldsmith">Jerry Goldsmith</a> with the <a href="/wiki/National_Philharmonic_Orchestra" title="National Philharmonic Orchestra">National Philharmonic Orchestra</a> and was released on <a href="/wiki/Var%C3%A8se_Sarabande" title="Varèse Sarabande">Varèse Sarabande</a> CDs shortly after Telarc's first theme release but before North's death. Eventually, a mono mix-down of North's original recordings, which had survived in the interim, would be released as a limited-edition CD by <a href="/wiki/Intrada_Records" title="Intrada Records">Intrada Records</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burt1995_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burt1995-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theatrical_run">Theatrical run</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Theatrical run">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The film's world <a href="/wiki/Premiere" title="Premiere">premiere</a> was on April 2, 1968, at the <a href="/wiki/Uptown_Theater_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Uptown Theater (Washington, D.C.)">Uptown Theater</a> in Washington, D.C. It opened two days later at the Warner Cinerama Theatre in Hollywood and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loew%27s_Capitol_Theatre" title="Loew's Capitol Theatre">Loew's Capitol</a> in New York. Kubrick then deleted nineteen minutes of footage from the film before its general release in five other U.S. cities on April 10, 1968, and internationally in five cities the following day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgel1970170_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgel1970170-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-underview_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-underview-130">[130]</a></sup> where it was shown in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/70mm" title="70mm">70mm</a> format, used a six-track stereo magnetic soundtrack, and projected in the 2.21:1 <a href="/wiki/Aspect_ratio" title="Aspect ratio">aspect ratio</a>. The general release of the film in its 35mm <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anamorphic" title="Anamorphic">anamorphic</a> format took place in autumn 1968 and used either a four-track magnetic stereo soundtrack or an optical monaural soundtrack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>The original seventy-millimetre release, like many Super Panavision 70 films of the era such as <i><a href="/wiki/Grand_Prix_(1966_film)" title="Grand Prix (1966 film)">Grand Prix</a></i>, was advertised as being in "Cinerama" in cinemas equipped with special projection optics and a deeply curved screen. In standard cinemas, the film was identified as a seventy-millimetre production. The original release of <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i> in seventy-millimetre Cinerama with six-track sound played continually for more than a year in several venues, and for one hundred and three weeks in Los Angeles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-in70mm_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-in70mm-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>The following year, <i>2001</i> was appointed by a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">United States Department of State</a> committee to be the American entry at the <a href="/wiki/6th_Moscow_International_Film_Festival" title="6th Moscow International Film Festival">6th Moscow International Film Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moscow1969_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moscow1969-133">[133]</a></sup> The film was re-released in 1974, 1977, and again in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hall-134">[134]</a></sup> Once 2001, the film's timeset, arrived, a restoration of the seventy-millimetre version was screened at the Ebert's Overlooked Film Festival, and the production was also reissued to selected film houses in North America, Europe and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_video">Home video</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Home video">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The film has been released in several forms:</p>
<ul>
<li>In 1980, MGM/CBS Home Video released the film on <a href="/wiki/VHS" title="VHS">VHS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Betamax" title="Betamax">Betamax</a> home video.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1983, it was released on <a href="/wiki/LaserDisc" title="LaserDisc">LaserDisc</a> by MGM in full screen.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>In 1987, it was released on VHS by MGM/UA Home Video.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>In 1989, <a href="/wiki/The_Criterion_Collection" title="The Criterion Collection">The Criterion Collection</a> released a 3-disc special <a href="/wiki/LaserDisc" title="LaserDisc">LaserDisc</a> edition with a transfer monitored by Kubrick himself.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>In 1997, MGM released the film on DVD.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>In 1999, it was re-released on VHS, and as part of the "Stanley Kubrick Collection" in both VHS format (1999) and DVD (2000) with remastered sound and picture. In some video releases, three <a href="/wiki/Intertitle" title="Intertitle">title cards</a> were added to the three "blank screen" moments; "OVERTURE" at the beginning, "ENTR'ACTE" during the intermission, and "EXIT MUSIC" after the closing credits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Additionally, the film has been released in high definition on both <a href="/wiki/HD_DVD" title="HD DVD">HD DVD</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray Disc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Box-office">Box-office</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Box-office">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The film earned $8.5 million in theatrical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_rental" title="Gross rental">gross rental</a> from <a href="/wiki/Roadshow_theatrical_release" title="Roadshow theatrical release">roadshow engagements</a> throughout 1968,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hall-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> contributing to North American rentals of $16.4 million and worldwide rentals of $21.9 million during its original release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block_.282010.29_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Block_.282010.29-141">[141]</a></sup> Reissues have brought its cumulative exhibition gross to $56.9 million in North America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mojo_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mojo-5">[5]</a></sup> and over $190 million worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_Corner_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_Corner-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reaction">Critical reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Critical reaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Upon release, <i>2001</i> polarized critical opinion, receiving both ecstatic praise and vehement derision. Some critics viewed the original 161-minute cut shown at premieres in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington D.C.</a>, New York, and Los Angeles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgel1970169_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgel1970169-1">[1]</a></sup> while others saw the nineteen-minute-shorter general release version that was in theatres from April 10, 1968 onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-underview_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-underview-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Penelope_Gilliatt" title="Penelope Gilliatt">Penelope Gilliatt</a> said it was "some kind of great film, and an unforgettable endeavor ... The film is hypnotically entertaining, and it is funny without once being gaggy, but it is also rather harrowing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilliatt_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilliatt-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Champlin" title="Charles Champlin">Charles Champlin</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> opined that it was "the picture that science fiction fans of every age and in every corner of the world have prayed (sometimes forlornly) that the industry might some day give them. It is an ultimate statement of the science fiction film, an awesome realization of the spatial future ... it is a milestone, a landmark for a spacemark, in the art of film."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Champlin_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Champlin-143">[143]</a></sup> Louise Sweeney of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Christian_Science_Monitor" title="The Christian Science Monitor">The Christian Science Monitor</a></i> felt that <i>2001</i> was "a brilliant intergalactic satire on modern technology. It's also a dazzling 160-minute tour on the Kubrick filmship through the universe out there beyond our earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweeney_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sweeney-144">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philip_French" title="Philip French">Philip French</a> wrote that the film was "perhaps the first multi-million-dollar supercolossal movie since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D.W._Griffith" title="D.W. Griffith">D.W. Griffith</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Intolerance_(film)" title="Intolerance (film)">Intolerance</a></i> fifty years ago which can be regarded as the work of one man ...<i>Space Odyssey</i> is important as the high-water mark of science-fiction movie making, or at least of the genre's futuristic branch."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-French-145">[145]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">The Boston Globe</a>'s</i> review indicated that it was "the world's most extraordinary film. Nothing like it has ever been shown in Boston before or, for that matter, anywhere ... The film is as exciting as the discovery of a new dimension in life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adams_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adams-146">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roger_Ebert" title="Roger Ebert">Roger Ebert</a> gave the film four stars in his original review, believing the film "succeeds magnificently on a cosmic scale."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebert_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebert-147">[147]</a></sup> He later put it on his Top 10 list for <a href="/wiki/Sight_%26_Sound" title="Sight &amp; Sound">Sight &amp; Sound</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> provided at least seven different mini-reviews of the film in various issues in 1968, each one slightly more positive than the preceding one; in the final review dated December 27, 1968, the magazine called <i>2001</i> "an epic film about the history and future of mankind, brilliantly directed by Stanley Kubrick. The special effects are mindblowing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unknown_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unknown-149">[149]</a></sup> Director <a href="/wiki/Martin_Scorsese" title="Martin Scorsese">Martin Scorsese</a> has also listed it as one of his favourite films of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Critic <a href="/wiki/David_Denby" title="David Denby">David Denby</a> later compared Kubrick to the monolith from <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i>, calling him "a force of supernatural intelligence, appearing at great intervals amid high-pitched shrieks, who gives the world a violent kick up the next rung of the evolutionary ladder".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pauline_Kael" title="Pauline Kael">Pauline Kael</a> said it was "a monumentally unimaginative movie",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kauffmann" title="Stanley Kauffmann">Stanley Kauffmann</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> called it "a film that is so dull, it even dulls our interest in the technical ingenuity for the sake of which Kubrick has allowed it to become dull."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Renata_Adler" title="Renata Adler">Renata Adler</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> wrote that it was "somewhere between hypnotic and immensely boring."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adler-154">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)"><i>Variety</i></a>'s 'Robe' believed the film was a "[b]ig, beautiful, but plodding sci-fi epic ... A major achievement in cinematography and special effects, <i>2001</i> lacks dramatic appeal to a large degree and only conveys suspense after the halfway mark."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Sarris" title="Andrew Sarris">Andrew Sarris</a> called it "one of the grimmest films I have ever seen in my life ...<i>2001</i> is a disaster because it is much too abstract to make its abstract points."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarris_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sarris-156">[156]</a></sup> (Sarris reversed his opinion upon a second viewing of the film, and declared, "<i>2001</i> is indeed a major work by a major artist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>) <a href="/wiki/John_Simon_(critic)" title="John Simon (critic)">John Simon</a> felt it was "a regrettable failure, although not a total one. This film is fascinating when it concentrates on apes or machines ... and dreadful when it deals with the in-betweens: humans ...<i>2001,</i> for all its lively visual and mechanical spectacle, is a kind of space-<i>Spartacus</i> and, more pretentious still, a <a href="/wiki/Shaggy_God_story" title="Shaggy God story">shaggy God story</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simon-158">[158]</a></sup> Eminent historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger,_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger, Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger, Jr.</a> deemed the film "morally pretentious, intellectually obscure and inordinately long ... a film out of control".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-myth_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-myth-159">[159]</a></sup> The BBC said that its slow pacing often alienates modern audiences more than it did upon its initial release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_fiction_writers">Science fiction writers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Science fiction writers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Science fiction writers had a range of reactions to the film. <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bradbury" title="Ray Bradbury">Ray Bradbury</a> praised the film's photography, but disliked the banality of most of the dialogue, and believed that the audience does not care when Poole dies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Both he and <a href="/wiki/Lester_del_Rey" title="Lester del Rey">Lester del Rey</a> were put off by the film's feeling of sterility and blandness in all the human encounters amidst all the technological wonders, while both praised the pictorial element of the film. Del Rey was especially harsh, describing the film as dull, confusing, and boring, predicting "[i]t will probably be a box-office disaster, too, and thus set major science-fiction movie making back another ten years." However, the film was praised by science-fiction novelist <a href="/wiki/Samuel_R._Delany" title="Samuel R. Delany">Samuel R. Delany</a> who was impressed by how the film undercuts the audience's normal sense of space and orientation in several ways. Like Bradbury, Delany picked up on the banality of the dialogue (in Delany's phrasing the characters are saying nothing meaningful), but Delany regards this as a dramatic strength, a prelude to the rebirth at the conclusion of the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Without analyzing the film in detail, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a> spoke well of <i>Space Odyssey</i> in his autobiography, and other essays. The film won the <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award" title="Hugo Award">Hugo Award</a> for best dramatic presentation, an award heavily voted on by science fiction fans and published science-fiction writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hugo_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-hugo-163">[163]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_P._Hogan_(writer)" title="James P. Hogan (writer)">James P. Hogan</a> liked the film but complained about the ending that didn't make any sense to him, leading to a bet about whether he could write something better or not; "I stole Arthur's plot idea shamelessly and produced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inherit_the_Stars" title="Inherit the Stars">Inherit the Stars</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_film">Influence on film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Influence on film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"Stanley Kubrick made the ultimate science fiction movie, and it is going to be very hard for someone to come along and make a better movie, as far as I'm concerned. On a technical level, it can be compared, but personally I think that '2001' is far superior."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—George Lucas, 1977<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-in70mm_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-in70mm-132">[132]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The influence of <i>2001</i> on subsequent filmmakers is considerable. <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">Steven Spielberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">George Lucas</a> and others, including many special effects technicians, discuss the impact the film has had on them in a featurette titled <i>Standing on the Shoulders of Kubrick: The Legacy of 2001</i>, included in the 2007 DVD release of the film. Spielberg calls it his film generation's "big bang", while Lucas says it was "hugely inspirational", labeling Kubrick as "the filmmaker's filmmaker". <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Pollack" title="Sydney Pollack">Sydney Pollack</a> refers to it as "groundbreaking", and <a href="/wiki/William_Friedkin" title="William Friedkin">William Friedkin</a> states <i>2001</i> is "the grandfather of all such films". At the 2007 Venice film festival, director <a href="/wiki/Ridley_Scott" title="Ridley Scott">Ridley Scott</a> stated he believed <i>2001</i> was the unbeatable film that in a sense killed the science fiction genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Similarly, film critic Michel Ciment in his essay "Odyssey of Stanley Kubrick" stated, "Kubrick has conceived a film which in one stroke has made the whole science fiction cinema obsolete."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> However, others credit <i>2001</i> with opening up a market for films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Close_Encounters_of_the_Third_Kind" title="Close Encounters of the Third Kind">Close Encounters of the Third Kind</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Alien_(film)" title="Alien (film)">Alien</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Blade_Runner" title="Blade Runner">Blade Runner</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Contact_(1997_American_film)" title="Contact (1997 American film)">Contact</a></i>; proving that big-budget "serious" science-fiction films can be commercially successful, and establishing the "sci-fi blockbuster" as a Hollywood staple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Science magazine <i>Discover</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s blogger Stephen Cass, discussing the considerable impact of the film on subsequent science-fiction, writes that "the balletic spacecraft scenes set to sweeping classical music, the tarantula-soft tones of <a href="/wiki/HAL_9000" title="HAL 9000">HAL 9000</a>, and the ultimate alien artifact, the Monolith, have all become enduring cultural icons in their own right."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_media">Influence on media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Influence on media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One commentator has suggested that the image of the Star Child and Earth has contributed to the rise of the "whole earth" icon as a symbol of the unity of humanity. Writing in <i>The Asia Pacific Journal</i> Robert Jacobs traces the history of this icon from early cartoons and drawings of Earth to photographs of Earth from early space missions, to its historic appearance on the cover of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Whole_Earth_Catalog" title="The Whole Earth Catalog">The Whole Earth Catalog</a></i>. Noting that images of the entire planet recur several times in <i>A Space Odyssey</i>, Jacobs writes</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>the most dramatic use of the icon was in the film's conclusion. In this scene ... Bowman is reborn as the Star Child ... depicted as a fetus floating in space in an <a href="/wiki/Amniotic_sac" title="Amniotic sac">amniotic sac</a>. The Star Child turns to consider the Whole Earth floating in front of it, both glowing a bright blue-white. The two appear as newborn versions of Man and Earth, face-to-face, ready to be born into a future of unthinkable possibilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_technology">Influence on technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Influence on technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2001interview.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="211" data-file-width="471" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7f/2001interview.png/220px-2001interview.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7f/2001interview.png/330px-2001interview.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7f/2001interview.png/440px-2001interview.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2001interview.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A scene where a tablet-style device is portrayed in the film</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In August 2011, in response to <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a>'s patent infringement lawsuit against <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a>, the latter argued that Apple's <a href="/wiki/IPad" title="IPad">iPad</a> was effectively modeled on the visual tablets that appear aboard spaceship <i>Discovery</i> in the <i>Space Odyssey</i> film, which constitute <a href="/wiki/Prior_art" title="Prior art">prior art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siri_(software)" title="Siri (software)">Siri</a>", Apple's natural language voice control system for the <a href="/wiki/IPhone_4S" title="IPhone 4S">iPhone 4S</a>, features a reference to the film: it responds "I'm sorry I can't do that" when asked to "open the pod bay doors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> When asked repeatedly, it may say, "Without your space helmet, you're going to find this rather... breathtaking."</p>
<p>Inspired by Clarke's visual tablet device, in 1994 a European Commission-funded R&amp;D project code named "NewsPAD" developed and pilot tested a portable 'multimedia viewer' aiming for the realisation of an electronic multimedia 'newspaper' pointing the way to a future fully interactive and highly personalised information source. Involved partners were <a href="/wiki/Acorn_Computers#NewsPad" title="Acorn Computers">Acorn RISC Technologies UK</a>, Archimedes GR, Carat FR, Ediciones Primera Plana ES, Institut Català de Tecnologia ES, and TechMAPP UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accolades_and_honors">Accolades and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Accolades and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">Awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>2001</i> earned Stanley Kubrick an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Visual_Effects" title="Academy Award for Visual Effects">Best Visual Effects</a>, as well as nominations for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Directing" title="Academy Award for Directing">Best Director</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Writing_Original_Screenplay" title="Academy Award for Writing Original Screenplay">Original Screenplay</a> (shared with Arthur C. Clarke). Anthony Masters was also nominated for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Art_Direction" title="Academy Award for Best Art Direction">Best Art Direction</a>. An honorary award was made to John Chambers in <a href="/wiki/41st_Academy_Awards" title="41st Academy Awards">that year</a> for his make-up work on <i><a href="/wiki/Planet_of_the_Apes" title="Planet of the Apes">Planet of the Apes</a></i>, and Clarke reports that he "wondered, as loudly as possible, whether the judges had passed over <i>2001</i> because they thought we had used real ape-men".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> The film won four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BAFTA_Awards" title="BAFTA Awards">Baftas</a>, for Art Direction, Cinematography, Sound Track and as Best Road Show, and was a nominee in the <a href="/wiki/BAFTA_Award_for_Best_Film" title="BAFTA Award for Best Film">Best Film</a> category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bafta_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-bafta-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Board_of_Review" title="National Board of Review">National Board of Review</a> listed <i>2001</i> among <a href="/wiki/National_Board_of_Review_Awards_1968" title="National Board of Review Awards 1968">the Top Ten Films of 1968</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Film_Critics_Circle_Awards_1968&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kansas City Film Critics Circle Awards 1968 (page does not exist)">Kansas City Film Critics</a> gave it both the Best Film and Best Director awards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Kubrick earned the <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award" title="Hugo Award">Hugo Award</a> for <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award_for_Best_Dramatic_Presentation" title="Hugo Award for Best Dramatic Presentation">Best Dramatic Presentation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hugo_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-hugo-163">[163]</a></sup> and was nominated for both the <a href="/wiki/Directors_Guild_of_America_Award" title="Directors Guild of America Award">Directors Guild of America Award</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Laurel_Awards" title="Laurel Awards">Laurel Award</a> (on which <i>2001</i> was named the Best Road Show of 1968).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laurel_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-laurel-178">[178]</a></sup> Both the <a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/C%C3%ADrculo_de_Escritores_Cinematogr%C3%A1ficos" title="es:Círculo de Escritores Cinematográficos">Cinema Writers Circle</a> of Spain and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_di_Donatello_Awards" title="David di Donatello Awards">David di Donatello Awards</a> in Italy named <i>2001</i> the best foreign production of 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Top_film_lists">Top film lists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Top film lists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>2001</i> was No. 15 on <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">AFI</a>'s 2007 <i><a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Movies_(10th_Anniversary_Edition)" title="AFI's 100 Years...100 Movies (10th Anniversary Edition)">100 Years ... 100 Movies</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> (22 in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years..._100_Movies" title="AFI's 100 Years... 100 Movies">1998</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> was named No. 40 on its <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years..._100_Thrills" title="AFI's 100 Years... 100 Thrills">100 Years, 100 Thrills</a>,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> was included on its <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years..._100_Movie_Quotes" title="AFI's 100 Years... 100 Movie Quotes">100 Years, 100 Quotes</a></i> (No. 78 "Open the pod bay doors, HAL."),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> and <i>Hal 9000</i> was the No. 13 villain in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years..._100_Heroes_and_Villains" title="AFI's 100 Years... 100 Heroes and Villains">100 Years... 100 Heroes and Villains</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> The film was also No. 47 on <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Cheers" title="AFI's 100 Years...100 Cheers">AFI's 100 Years...100 Cheers</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> and the No. 1 science fiction film on <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_10_Top_10" title="AFI's 10 Top 10">AFI's 10 Top 10</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>2001</i> is the only science fiction film to make the <i><a href="/wiki/Sight_%26_Sound" title="Sight &amp; Sound">Sight &amp; Sound</a></i> poll for ten best films, and tops the <a href="/wiki/Online_Film_Critics_Society" title="Online Film Critics Society">Online Film Critics Society</a> list of "greatest science fiction films of all time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> In 1991, this film was deemed "culturally, historically, or aesthetically significant" by the United States <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> and selected for preservation in the <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Registry" title="National Film Registry">National Film Registry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Other lists that include the film are <i><a href="/wiki/50_Films_to_See_Before_You_Die" title="50 Films to See Before You Die">50 Films to See Before You Die</a></i> (#6), The Village Voice 100 Best Films of the 20th century (#11), the <i>Sight &amp; Sound</i> Top Ten poll (#6),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> and Roger Ebert's Top Ten (1968) (#2). In 1995, the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican</a> named it as one of the 45 best films ever made (and included it in a sub-list of the "Top Ten Art Movies" of all time.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, the film was the third most screened film in secondary schools in the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCSchools_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCSchools-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretation">Interpretation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Interpretation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interpretations_of_2001:_A_Space_Odyssey" title="Interpretations of 2001: A Space Odyssey">Interpretations of 2001: A Space Odyssey</a></div>
<p>Since its premiere, <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i> has been analyzed and interpreted by professional film critics, amateur writers and science fiction fans, virtually all of whom have mentioned its deliberate ambiguity. Questions about <i>2001</i> range from uncertainty about its deeper philosophical implications about humanity's origins and final destiny in the universe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> to interpreting elements of the film's more enigmatic scenes such as the meaning of the monolith, or the final fate of astronaut David Bowman. There are also simpler and more mundane questions about what drives the plot, in particular the causes of Hal's breakdown (explained in earlier drafts but kept mysterious in the film).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stanley Kubrick encouraged people to explore their own interpretations of the film and refused to offer an explanation of "what really happened" in the film, preferring instead to let audiences embrace their own ideas and theories. In a 1968 interview with <a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy"><i>Playboy</i> magazine</a>, Kubrick stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>You're free to speculate as you wish about the philosophical and <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorical</a> meaning of the film—and such speculation is one indication that it has succeeded in gripping the audience at a deep level—but I don't want to spell out a verbal road map for <i>2001</i> that every viewer will feel obligated to pursue or else fear he's missed the point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Playboy_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Playboy-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In a subsequent discussion of the film with Joseph Gelmis, Kubrick said his main aim was to avoid "intellectual verbalization" and reach "the viewer's subconscious." However, he said he did not deliberately strive for ambiguity—it was simply an inevitable outcome of making the film nonverbal, though he acknowledged this ambiguity was an invaluable asset to the film. He was willing then to give a fairly straightforward explanation of the plot on what he called the "simplest level," but unwilling to discuss the metaphysical interpretation of the film which he felt should be left up to the individual viewer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>For some readers, Arthur C. Clarke's more straightforward novelization of the script is key to interpreting the film. Clarke's novel explicitly identifies the monolith as a tool created by an alien race that has been through many stages of evolution, moving from organic form to biomechanical, and finally achieving a state of pure energy. These aliens travel the cosmos assisting lesser species to take evolutionary steps. Conversely, film critic Penelope Houston wrote in 1971 that because the novel differs in many key respects from the film, it perhaps should not be regarded as the skeleton key to unlock it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>Multiple allegorical interpretations of <i>2001</i> have been proposed, including seeing it as a commentary on Friedrich Nietzsche's philosophical tract <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i>, or as an allegory of human conception, birth and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> The latter can be seen through the final moments of the film, which are defined by the image of the "star child," an <a href="/wiki/In_utero" title="In utero">in utero</a> fetus that draws on the work of <a href="/wiki/Lennart_Nilsson" title="Lennart Nilsson">Lennart Nilsson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgoot2006p339_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burgoot2006p339-198">[198]</a></sup> The star child signifies a "great new beginning,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgoot2006p339_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burgoot2006p339-198">[198]</a></sup> and is depicted naked and ungirded, but with its eyes wide open.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> Leonard F. Wheat sees <i>Space Odyssey</i> as a multi-layered allegory, commenting simultaneously on Nietzsche, Homer, and the relationship of man to machine.</p>
<p>The reasons for Hal's malfunction and subsequent malignant behavior have also elicited much discussion. He has been compared to Frankenstein's monster. In Clarke's novel, Hal malfunctions because of being ordered to lie to the crew of <i>Discovery</i> and withhold confidential information from them, despite being constructed for "the accurate processing of information without distortion or concealment". Film critic Roger Ebert wrote that Hal, as the supposedly perfect computer, actually behaves in the most human fashion of all of the characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roger_Ebert_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roger_Ebert-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Rolling Stone</i> reviewer Bob McClay sees the film as like a four-movement symphony, its story told with "deliberate realism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Carolyn Geduld believes that what "structurally unites all four episodes of the film" is the monolith, the film's largest and most unresolvable enigma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Vincent LoBrutto's biography of Kubrick says that for many, Clarke's novel is the key to understanding the monolith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> Similarly, Geduld observes that "the monolith ... has a very simple explanation in Clarke's novel," though she later asserts that even the novel doesn't fully explain the ending.</p>
<p>McClay's <i>Rolling Stone</i> review describes a parallelism between the monolith's first appearance in which tool usage is imparted to the apes (thus 'beginning' mankind) and the completion of "another evolution" in the fourth and final encounter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> with the monolith. In a similar vein, Tim Dirks ends his synopsis saying "[t]he cyclical evolution from ape to man to spaceman to angel-starchild-superman is complete."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filmsite.org_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-filmsite.org-204">[204]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first and second encounters of humanity with the monolith have visual elements in common; both apes, and later astronauts, touch the monolith gingerly with their hands, and both sequences conclude with near-identical images of the Sun appearing directly over the monolith (the first with a crescent moon adjacent to it in the sky, the second with a near-identical crescent Earth in the same position), both echoing the Sun–Earth–Moon <a href="/wiki/Syzygy_(astronomy)" title="Syzygy (astronomy)">alignment</a> seen at the very beginning of the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> The second encounter also suggests the triggering of the monolith's radio signal to Jupiter by the presence of humans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> echoing the premise of Clarke's source story "The Sentinel".</p>
<p>The monolith is the subject of the film's final line of dialogue (spoken at the end of the "Jupiter Mission" segment): "Its origin and purpose still a total mystery." Reviewers McClay and Roger Ebert wrote that the monolith is the main element of mystery in the film; Ebert described "the shock of the monolith's straight edges and square corners among the weathered rocks," and the apes warily circling it as prefiguring man reaching "for the stars."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebert_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-ebert-147">[147]</a></sup> Patrick Webster suggests the final line relates to how the film should be approached as a whole, noting "The line appends not merely to the discovery of the monolith on the Moon, but to our understanding of the film in the light of the ultimate questions it raises about the mystery of the universe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>The film conveys what some viewers have described as a sense of the <a href="/wiki/Sublime_(philosophy)" title="Sublime (philosophy)">sublime</a> and <a href="/wiki/Numinous" title="Numinous">numinous</a>. Roger Ebert writes in his essay on <i>2001</i> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Movies" title="The Great Movies">The Great Movies</a></i>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>North's [rejected] score, which is available on a recording, is a good job of film composition, but would have been wrong for <i>2001</i> because, like all scores, it attempts to underline the action—to give us emotional cues. The classical music chosen by Kubrick exists outside the action. It uplifts. It wants to be sublime; it brings a seriousness and transcendence to the visuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roger_Ebert_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-Roger_Ebert-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In a book on architecture, Gregory Caicco writes that <i>Space Odyssey</i> illustrates how our quest for space is motivated by two contradictory desires, a "desire for the sublime" characterized by a need to encounter something totally other than ourselves—"something numinous"—and the conflicting desire for a beauty that makes us feel no longer "lost in space," but at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Similarly, an article in <i>The Greenwood Encyclopedia of Science Fiction and Fantasy</i>, titled "Sense of Wonder," describes how <i>2001</i> creates a "numinous sense of wonder" by portraying a universe that inspires a sense of awe, which at the same time we feel we can understand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> Christopher Palmer wrote that there exists in the film a coexistence of "the sublime and the banal," as the film implies that to get into space, mankind had to suspend the "sense of wonder" that motivated him to explore space to begin with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sequels_and_adaptations">Sequels and adaptations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Sequels and adaptations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Kubrick did not envision a sequel to <i>2001.</i> Fearing the later exploitation and recycling of his material in other productions (as was done with the props from MGM's <i><a href="/wiki/Forbidden_Planet" title="Forbidden Planet">Forbidden Planet</a></i>), he ordered all sets, props, miniatures, production blueprints, and prints of unused scenes destroyed. Most of these materials were lost, with some exceptions: a <i>2001</i> spacesuit backpack appeared in the "Close Up" episode of the Gerry Anderson series <i><a href="/wiki/UFO_(TV_series)" title="UFO (TV series)">UFO</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgel1970169_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgel1970169-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bizony_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bizony-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2010featurette_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-2010featurette-211">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> and one of Hal's eyepieces is in the possession of the author of <i>Hal's Legacy</i>, David G. Stork. In 2012 Lockheed engineer Adam Johnson, working with <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I._Ordway_III" title="Frederick I. Ordway III">Frederick I. Ordway III</a>, science adviser to Kubrick, wrote the book <i>2001: The Lost Science</i>, which for the first time featured many of the blueprints of the spacecraft and film sets that previously had been thought destroyed.</p>
<p>Clarke wrote three sequel novels: <i><a href="/wiki/2010:_Odyssey_Two" title="2010: Odyssey Two">2010: Odyssey Two</a></i> (1982), <i><a href="/wiki/2061:_Odyssey_Three" title="2061: Odyssey Three">2061: Odyssey Three</a></i> (1987), and <i><a href="/wiki/3001:_The_Final_Odyssey" title="3001: The Final Odyssey">3001: The Final Odyssey</a></i> (1997). The only filmed sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/2010_(film)" title="2010 (film)">2010</a></i>, was based on Clarke's 1982 novel and was released in 1984. Kubrick was not involved in the production of this film, which was directed by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hyams" title="Peter Hyams">Peter Hyams</a> in a more conventional style with more dialogue. Clarke saw it as a fitting adaptation of his novel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> and had a brief <a href="/wiki/Cameo_appearance" title="Cameo appearance">cameo appearance</a> in the film. As Kubrick had ordered all models and blueprints from <i>2001</i> destroyed, Hyams was forced to recreate these models from scratch for <i>2010</i>. Hyams also claimed that he would not have made the film had he not received both Kubrick's and Clarke's blessings:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I had a long conversation with Stanley and told him what was going on. If it met with his approval, I would do the film; and if it didn't, I wouldn't. I certainly would not have thought of doing the film if I had not gotten the blessing of Kubrick. He's one of my idols; simply one of the greatest talents that's ever walked the Earth. He more or less said, "Sure. Go do it. I don't care." And another time he said, "Don't be afraid. Just go do your own movie."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The other two novels have not been adapted for the screen, although actor <a href="/wiki/Tom_Hanks" title="Tom Hanks">Tom Hanks</a> has expressed interest in possible adaptations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012, two screenplay adaptations of both <i>2061</i> and <i>3001</i> were posted on the 2001:Exhibit website, in the hopes of generating interest in both MGM and Warner Bros. to adapt the last two novels into films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning in 1976, <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">Marvel Comics</a> published a <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(comics)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (comics)">comic adaptation</a> of the film written and drawn by <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kirby" title="Jack Kirby">Jack Kirby</a>, and a 10-issue monthly series expanding on the ideas of the film and novel, also created by Kirby.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parodies_and_homages">Parodies and homages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Parodies and homages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>2001</i> has been the frequent subject of both <a href="/wiki/Parody" title="Parody">parody</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homage_(arts)" title="Homage (arts)">homage</a>, sometimes extensively and other times briefly, employing both its distinctive music and iconic imagery.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_advertising_and_print">In advertising and print</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: In advertising and print">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mad_(magazine)" title="Mad (magazine)">Mad</a></i> magazine #125 (March 1969) featured a spoof called <i>201 Minutes of a Space Idiocy</i> written by <a href="/wiki/Dick_DeBartolo" title="Dick DeBartolo">Dick DeBartolo</a> and illustrated by <a href="/wiki/Mort_Drucker" title="Mort Drucker">Mort Drucker</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> In the final panels it is revealed that the monolith is a film script titled "'How to Make an Incomprehensible Science Fiction Movie' by Stanley Kubrick". It was reprinted in various special issues, in the <i>MAD About the Sixties</i> book, and partially in the book "The Making of Kubrick's 2001".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup></li>
<li>The August 1971 album <i><a href="/wiki/Who%27s_Next" title="Who's Next">Who's Next</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a> featured as its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cover_artwork" title="Cover artwork">cover artwork</a> a photograph of a concrete slab at <a href="/wiki/Easington_Colliery" title="Easington Colliery">Easington Colliery</a> with the band apparently doing up their trouser zips. The decision to photograph this "monolith" image while on their way to a concert followed discussion between <a href="/wiki/John_Entwistle" title="John Entwistle">John Entwistle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keith_Moon" title="Keith Moon">Keith Moon</a> about Kubrick's film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></li>
<li>Thought to be the first time Kubrick gave permission for his work to be re-used, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a>'s 1999 website advertisement "It was a bug, Dave" was made using footage from the film. Launched during the era of concerns over Y2K bugs, the ad implied that Hal's weird behavior was caused by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Y2K" title="Y2K">Y2K</a> bug, before driving home the point that "only Macintosh was designed to function perfectly".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_film_and_television">In film and television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: In film and television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mel_Brooks" title="Mel Brooks">Mel Brooks</a>' satirical film <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_World,_Part_I" title="History of the World, Part I">History of the World, Part I</a></i> opens with a parody of Kubrick's "Dawn of Man" sequence, followed by the parody of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_Million_Years_BC" title="One Million Years BC">One Million Years BC</a></i>, narrated by <a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">Orson Welles</a>. DVDVerdict describes this parody as "spot on".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> A similar spoof of the "Dawn of Man" sequence also opened Ken Shapiro's 1974 comedy <i><a href="/wiki/The_Groove_Tube" title="The Groove Tube">The Groove Tube</a></i> in which the monolith was replaced by a television set. (The film is mostly a parody of television. <i>Film and Filming</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> held that after this wonderful opening, the film slid downhill.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Woody_Allen" title="Woody Allen">Woody Allen</a> cast actor <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Rain" title="Douglas Rain">Douglas Rain</a> (Hal in Kubrick's film) in an uncredited part as the voice of the controlling computer in the closing sequences of his science-fiction comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Sleeper_(1973_film)" title="Sleeper (1973 film)">Sleeper</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Matt_Groening" title="Matt Groening">Matt Groening</a>'s animated series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Simpsons" title="The Simpsons">The Simpsons</a></i>, of which Kubrick was a fan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Futurama" title="Futurama">Futurama</a></i> frequently reference <i>2001</i>, along with other Kubrick films. <i>The Simpsons</i> had in the episode "<a href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Homer" title="Deep Space Homer">Deep Space Homer</a>" <a href="/wiki/Bart_Simpson" title="Bart Simpson">Bart</a> throwing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Felt-tip_marker" title="Felt-tip marker">felt-tip marker</a> into the air; in slow motion it rotates, before a match cut replaces it with a cylindrical satellite. In 2004 <i>Empire</i> magazine listed this as the third best film parody of the entire run of the show.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> In the <i>Futurama</i> episode "<a href="/wiki/Love_and_Rocket" title="Love and Rocket">Love and Rocket</a>" a sentient spaceship revolts in a manner similar to Hal. <i>Games Radar</i> listed this as number 17 in its list of 20 Funniest Futurama parodies, while noting that <i>Futurama</i> has referenced <i>Space Odyssey</i> on several other occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the 2000 <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park" title="South Park">South Park</a></i> episode "<a href="/wiki/Trapper_Keeper_(South_Park)" title="Trapper Keeper (South Park)">Trapper Keeper</a>", an interaction between <a href="/wiki/Eric_Cartman" title="Eric Cartman">Eric Cartman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyle_Broflovski" title="Kyle Broflovski">Kyle Broflovski</a> parodies the conversation between Hal and Bowman within the inner core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peter_Sellers" title="Peter Sellers">Peter Sellers</a> starred in <a href="/wiki/Hal_Ashby" title="Hal Ashby">Hal Ashby</a>'s comedy-drama <i><a href="/wiki/Being_There" title="Being There">Being There</a></i> about a simple-minded middle-aged gardener who has lived his entire life in the townhouse of his wealthy employer. In the scene where he first leaves the house and ventures into the wide world for the first time, the soundtrack plays a jazzy version of Strauss' <i>Also Sprach Zarathustra</i> arranged by <a href="/wiki/Eumir_Deodato" title="Eumir Deodato">Eumir Deodato</a>. Film critic James A. Davidson writing for the film journal <i>Images</i> suggests "When Chance emerges from his home into the world, Ashby suggests his childlike nature by using Richard Strauss' <i>Thus Spake Zarathustra</i> as ironic background music, linking his hero with Kubrick's star baby in his masterpiece <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tim_Burton" title="Tim Burton">Tim Burton</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_and_the_Chocolate_Factory_(film)" title="Charlie and the Chocolate Factory (film)">Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</a></i> has a scene (using actual footage from <i>A Space Odyssey</i>) in which the monolith morphs into a chocolate bar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> <i>Catholic News</i> wrote that the film "had subtle and obvious riffs on everything from the saccharine Disney "Small World" exhibit to Munchkinland to, most brilliantly, a hilarious takeoff on Kubrick's <i>2001: A Space Odyssey.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Stanton" title="Andrew Stanton">Andrew Stanton</a>, the director of <i><a href="/wiki/WALL-E" title="WALL-E">WALL-E</a></i>, revealed in an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_(magazine)" title="Wired (magazine)">Wired</a></i> magazine that his film was in many ways his homage to <i>Space Odyssey</i>, <i>Alien</i>, <i>Blade Runner</i>, <i>Close Encounters</i> and several other science-fiction films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> The reviewer for <i>USA Today</i> described the resemblance of the spaceship's computer, Auto, to Hal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> The same year saw the release of the much less successful film <i><a href="/wiki/Eagle_Eye" title="Eagle Eye">Eagle Eye</a></i>, about which <i>The Charlotte Observer</i> said that, like <i>2001</i>, it featured a "red-eyed, calm-voiced supercomputer that took human life to protect what it felt were higher objectives"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></li>
<li>Commenting on the broader use of Ligeti's music beyond that by Kubrick, <i>London Magazine</i> in 2006 mentioned <a href="/wiki/Monty_Python" title="Monty Python">Monty Python</a>'s use of Ligeti in a 60-second spoof of <i>Space Odyssey</i> in the <i>Flying Circus</i> episode commonly labeled "A Book at Bedtime".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup></li>
<li>The poorly reviewed Canadian spoof <i><a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Travesty" title="2001: A Space Travesty">2001: A Space Travesty</a></i> has been occasionally alluded to as a full parody of Kubrick's film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> both because of its title and star Leslie Nielsen's many previous films which were full parodies of other films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> However, <i>Space Travesty</i> only makes occasional references to Kubrick's material, its "celebrities are really aliens" jokes resembling those in <i><a href="/wiki/Men_in_Black_(film)" title="Men in Black (film)">Men in Black</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> Canadian reviewer Jim Slotek said, "It's not really a spoof of <i>2001</i>, or anything in particular. There's a brief homage at the start, and one scene in a shuttle en route to the Moon that uses <i>The Blue Danube</i>... The rest is a patched together plot."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> Among many complaints about the film, reviewer Berge Garabedian derided the lack of much substantive connection to the Kubrick film (the latter of which he said was "funnier").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup></li>
<li>Among spoof references to several science-fiction films and shows,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airplane_II" title="Airplane II">Airplane II</a></i> features a computer called ROK 9000 in control of a Moon shuttle which malfunctions and kills crew members, which several reviewers found reminiscent of Hal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mystery_Science_Theater_3000" title="Mystery Science Theater 3000">Mystery Science Theater 3000</a></i> had the design of its main setting, the starship Satellite of Love, based on the bone-shaped satellite featured in the match cut from prehistoria to the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> The one-eyed design of the robot <a href="/wiki/Gypsy_(Mystery_Science_Theater_3000)" title="Gypsy (Mystery Science Theater 3000)">Gypsy</a> led the show to do various scenes comparing it to HAL 9000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> including a scene the <a href="/wiki/Mystery_Science_Theater_3000:_The_Movie" title="Mystery Science Theater 3000: The Movie">1996 feature film</a>, where the opening featuring <a href="/wiki/Michael_J._Nelson" title="Michael J. Nelson">Mike Nelson</a> jogging along the walls of the Satellite of Love parodies the scene where Frank Poole does the same in the <i>Discovery</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_software_and_video_games">In software and video games</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: In software and video games">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Video game director <a href="/wiki/Hideo_Kojima" title="Hideo Kojima">Hideo Kojima</a> has also cited <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i> as his favorite movie of all time and is frequently referenced in the <i><a href="/wiki/Metal_Gear" title="Metal Gear">Metal Gear</a></i> series; <a href="/wiki/Otacon" title="Otacon">Otacon</a> is named after Hal and <a href="/wiki/Solid_Snake" title="Solid Snake">Solid Snake</a>'s real name is Dave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<li><cite class="citation book">Pina, Leslie A. (2002). <i>Herman Miller Office</i>. <li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">(Usually translated as "Thus Spake Zarathustra" or occasionally "Thus Spoke Zarathustra" - The book by Nietzsche has been translated both ways and the title of Strauss's music is usually rendered in the original German whenever not discussed in the context of <i>2001</i>. Although Britannica Online's entry lists the piece as <i>spoke</i> Zarathustra, music encyclopedias usually go with 'spake'. Overall, 'spake' is more common mentioning the Strauss music and 'spoke' more common mentioning the book by Nietzsche. - the soundtrack album gives the former, the movie's credits give the latter).</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-:0-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:0_118-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:0_118-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.visual-memory.co.uk/amk/doc/brown3.html" rel="nofollow">"2001's Pre- and Post-Premiere Edits by Thomas E Brown"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 27,</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3A2001%3A+A+Space+Odyssey+%28film%29&amp;rft.btitle=2001%27s+Pre-+and+Post-Premiere+Edits+by+Thomas+E+Brown&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.visual-memory.co.uk%2Famk%2Fdoc%2Fbrown3.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Kubrick and editor Ray Lovejoy edited the film between April 5 and 9, 1968. Detailed instructions were sent to theatre owners already showing the film so that they could execute the specified trims themselves. This meant that some of the cuts may have been poorly done in a particular theatre, possibly causing the version seen by viewers early in the film's run to vary from theatre to theatre.</span></li>
